        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b13096"><meta property="og:title" content="Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/acsami.7b13096.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Owing to the high theoretical sodiation capacities, intermetallic alloy anodes have attracted considerable interest as electrodes for next-generation sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Here, we demonstrate the fabrication of intermetallic Fe–Sb alloy anode for SIBs via a high-throughput and industrially viable melt-spinning process. The earth-abundant and low-cost Fe–Sb-based alloy anode exhibits excellent cycling stability with nearly 466 mAh g–1 sodiation capacity at a specific current of 50 mA g–1 with 95% capacity retention after 80 cycles. Moreover, the alloy anode displayed outstanding rate performance with ∼300 mAh g–1 sodiation capacity at 1 A g–1. The crystalline features of the melt-spun fibers aid in the exceptional electrochemical performance of the alloy anode. Further, the feasibility of the alloy anode for real-life applications was demonstrated in a sodium-ion full-cell configuration which could deliver a sodiation capacity of over 300 mAh g–1 (based on anode) at 50 mA g–1 with more than 99% Coulombic efficiency. The results further exhort the prospects of melt-spun alloy anodes to realize fully functional sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/acsami.7b13096.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93188779c8ae54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188779c8ae54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '52e11f1d-e5a2-429e-9854-81ca01e58b85'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188779c8ae54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="93188779c8ae54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Intermetallic Alloy Anode for Performance Enhanced Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13096", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">47</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Owing to the high theoretical sodiation capacities, intermetallic alloy anodes have attracted considerable interest as electrodes for next-generation sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Here, we demonstrate the fabrication of intermetallic Fe–Sb alloy anode for SIBs via a high-throughput and industrially viable melt-spinning process. The earth-abundant and low-cost Fe–Sb-based alloy anode exhibits excellent cycling stability with nearly 466 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> sodiation capacity at a specific current of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with 95% capacity retention after 80 cycles. Moreover, the alloy anode displayed outstanding rate performance with ∼300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> sodiation capacity at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The crystalline features of the melt-spun fibers aid in the exceptional electrochemical performance of the alloy anode. Further, the feasibility of the alloy anode for real-life applications was demonstrated in a sodium-ion full-cell configuration which could deliver a sodiation capacity of over 300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on anode) at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with more than 99% Coulombic efficiency. The results further exhort the prospects of melt-spun alloy anodes to realize fully functional sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.

                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_battery">battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Alarming depletion and related aggravating environmental issues of fossil fuels call for the implementation of sustainable energy resources, such as solar, wind and wave. In this regard, it is vital to develop efficient, low-cost electrochemical energy storage systems (EES) to facilitate the large-scale implementation of these renewable energy resources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> The predominant lithium-ion battery (LIB) technology seems to be inadequate to meet the skyrocketing demands, as the existing lithium reserves are sparse and limited-calling for “beyond-LIB” research. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3, 4)</a> In this context, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received a renewed interest in the recent past in view of the abundant and low-cost of active materials (sodium), as well as the close similarity in operation mechanisms to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5, 6)</a> However, the larger size and mass of Na presents the challenge of finding suitable insertion hosts and the higher redox potential projects ∼15% penalty in energy density compared to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref7" aria-label="reference 2 and 7">(2, 7)</a> Nonetheless, recently significant progress has been made in the development of suitable SIB cathodes with performance at par with the LIB cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6, 8 and 9">(6, 8, 9)</a> However, the research progress to find a suitable anode capable of performance in full-cell configuration is still slow-paced.</div><div class="NLM_p">The ideal Na metal anode poses serious safety hazards because of its low melting point (97.7 °C) and high reactivity along with issues of dendrite formation that can lead to short circuit. In addition, graphite, the conventional anode for LIBs, is found to be unsuitable for SIBs, while nongraphitic hard carbons have been shown to accommodate Na ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10-12)</a> However, the sodiation potential of these disordered carbons are minimal, which is close to that of metallic sodium, causing further safety concerns such as plating and dendrite formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Moreover, the low tap-density of these carbon materials would severely lower the volumetric energy density- impeding the application in practical SIBs. Insertion anodes also suffer from limited insertion sites for the Na ions, which would lead to limited sodiation capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14, 15)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">On the other hand, alloy anodes such as tin, antimony and phosphorus possess high theoretical sodiation capacities at appropriate potential and are expected to aid the commercialization of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> However, these alloy anodes possess the challenges of huge volume changes during cycling- leading to pulverization and abrupt capacity fade. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 and 16">(13, 16)</a> Antimony-based anodes have been investigated as SIB anodes due to the high sodiation capacity of 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and ideal sodiation potential to eliminate dendrite formation and safety issues. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17, 18)</a> Many approaches have been undertaken to mitigate the issues of poor cycling stability of bare Sb anode, including the nanostructuring of active material, use of a carbon matrix, active/inactive buffer in the form of intermetallics, and so on. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 19 to 24">(19-24)</a> However, most of these strategies seem to be inappropriate for practical applications owing to their poor-scalability and throughput. Our approach was to develop a high-performance anode material for SIBs through a commercial process which could further find its potential in full-cell configurations.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we present large-scale and industrially viable melt-spinning process based production of intermetallic Fe–Sb alloy ribbons and investigate the electrochemical performance of these new alloys in sodium-ion batteries. Melt-spinning is a scalable, high-yield, rapid solidification process with high quench rates of ∼10<sup>6</sup> K s<sup>–1</sup>, which imparts the alloys with nanocrystalline/amorphous features and intriguing material traits. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25, 26)</a> The Fe–Sb alloy anodes exhibited outstanding cycling performance along with the exceptional rate capability. In addition, a full-cell configuration was also fabricated and its electrochemical studies were carried out to demonstrate the potential of these Fe–Sb alloy anodes for real-world applications. The preliminary electrochemical results suggest the suitability of these intermetallic Fe–Sb alloy anodes for practical next-generation performance enhanced SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The fabrication of the Fe–Sb alloy ribbons via melt-spinning is schematically illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information section 1.1</a>. Briefly, the Fe and Sb metal powders are intimately mixed in a mortar and pestle and then cold pressed into a pellet. The pellet is then loaded in a quartz tube with slit nozzle and melted via induction heating in argon atmosphere. The melt is forced down the quartz tube nozzle by pressurized argon gas and the alloy ribbons are formed when the melt falls upon the rotating water-cooled copper wheel. The melt-spinning process can deliver high yields of these alloy ribbons, making the process highly scalable.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. (a) Graphical Illustration of the Preparation of Melt-Spun Fe–Sb Alloy Ribbons, (b) Digital Images, and (c) Microscopic Images Showing Appearance of Freshly Prepared Alloy Ribbons</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Although iron is electrochemically inactive with respect to sodium, it can serve as a highly conductive matrix to host antimony and aid in the buffering of volume changes during the sodiation and desodiation processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27, 28)</a> Fe–Sb alloy was prepared via melt spinning with Fe:Sb 1:2 molar ratio and is hereafter denoted as MS_FeSb. Alloy ribbons of dimensions ∼20 × 0.8 × 0.05 mm (length × width × thickness) were obtained with more than 90% yield in the melt-spinning process. The melt-spun Fe–Sb alloy ribbons were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The sharp diffraction peaks attest the crystallinity of the alloy ribbons and the absence of any oxide impurities is also confirmed from the diffraction pattern. Furthermore, the diffraction peaks were Rietveld refined to analyze the phases present in the alloy ribbons. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows FeSb<sub>2</sub> and FeSb crystal structures and based on the obtained data the alloy composition was characterized and identified with a composition of 88.38% FeSb<sub>2</sub> and 11.62% FeSb, respectively (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD pattern of the as-obtained Fe–Sb alloy ribbons (PDF numbers 01-075-1498 34-1184 (FeSb<sub>2</sub>) and 34-1053 (FeSb)). (b) Crystal structures of FeSb<sub>2</sub> and FeSb alloys. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> (c) Phase composition of the melt-spun alloy.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Next, we examined the surface topography of the Fe–Sb alloy ribbons using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) and field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a and b shows HR-TEM images of flakes of Fe–Sb alloy. Analysis of an individual block shows lattice fringes with <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.285 nm corresponding to the orthorhombic phase of FeSb<sub>2</sub> alloy (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). A nanocrystalline configuration of Fe–Sb could be generated via rapid cooling (∼10<sup>4</sup>–10<sup>7</sup> K s<sup>–1</sup>) of the molten composite during the melt-spinning process which effectively arrests further nucleation and grain growth. In contrast to the conventional solution-based growth processes and strategies intended for nanostructuring of active materials, melt-spinning can aid in the rapid processing of crystalline alloy segments with comparatively higher yield. Furthermore, FE-SEM images of the flakes with ∼0.76 mm width (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>) which were in contact with the copper wheel (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>) appeared smooth (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a and b</a>) while the exposed surface appeared irregular (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c and d</a>). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c-g shows images corresponding to the energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) analysis of a single flake of Fe–Sb alloy on silicon substrate. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e show uniform distribution of Sb (labeled blue) and Fe (labeled as red)  <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h shows the corresponding EDX spectra of elements upon scanning a selected area on the surface of Fe–Sb flakes.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a, b) HR-TEM images of MS_Fe–Sb alloy flakes. (c–e) SEM-EDX micrographs showing mapping of the flakes (blue label represents Sb; red label represents Fe). (f) Elemental map obtained for a selected area.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the MS_Fe–Sb alloy ribbons was preliminarily evaluated in Na-ion half-cell assembly. Prior to cell assembly, the electrodes were prepared by a slurry-coating technique on copper foil (the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information section 1.3</a>). The electrodes were punched out into circular discs of ∼14 mm diameter (∼1.5–2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> active mass loading) and assembled into CR2016 coin cells with Na metal as the counter electrode and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte. For comparison, a set of Fe–Sb metal powders physically mixed electrodes were also investigated for the cycling stability. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figures 3">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>(a) shows the capacity retention plot and the MS_Fe–Sb alloy ribbons outperformed the physically mixed electrode counterparts in terms of cycling stability. The MS_FeSb alloy delivered a sodiation capacity of ∼555 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the first cycle with initial Coulombic efficiency of 81.3% and maintained 97–99% Coulombic efficiency in the subsequent cycles. Interestingly, the alloy anode retained ∼95% of the second sodiation capacity after 80 cycles. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows comparison of cycling performance delivered by pristine Sb, MS_Fe–Sb alloy and its physically mixed counter parts. Thus, the performance enhancement observed in MS_Fe–Sb alloy electrode could be attributed to the crystalline features inherited during intermetallic alloy formation through the melt-spinning process. In addition to this, the melt spun Fe–Sb electrodes exhibited excellent rate performances (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) with sodiation capacities of ∼300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at high current rate of 1 Ag<sup>1–</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows corresponding galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves). This further attests the highly conductive milieu and the fast diffusion pathways realized by high-temperature alloying of active materials of the electrode by means of the melt-spinning process.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Galvanostatic cycling at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Rate performance of MS_FeSb at different specific currents from 50 to 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cyclic voltammogram of MS_FeSb at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the potential window 0.005–2.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cyclic voltammetry (CV) studies as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> demonstrate the enhanced sodiation/desodiation behavior of the MS_Fe–Sb alloys over the pristine Sb powder. During the first sodiation process, both MS_Fe–Sb alloy as well as pristine Sb exhibited a prominent peak at ∼0.25 V vs Na for Sb and close to 5 mV vs Na for MS_Fe–Sb. This can be attributed to the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase and the initial sodiation of the active material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> In the following desodiation process, a prominent peak was observed at ∼1 V vs Na for Sb and ∼0.86 V vs Na for MS_Fe–Sb corresponding to the dealloying process. The overlapping peaks during the subsequent cycles attest the improved stability of the MS_Fe–Sb anode when compared to the rapid fading observed in the case of pure Sb electrode. After the initial formation cycles, the alloy exhibited three sodiation peaks at ∼0.64, 0.47, 0.33 V vs Na. and the peak currents were found to increase in the consequent cycles. This is a classical observation in alloy and conversion type materials and can be attributed to the decrease of the particle size and restructuration of the active material during the first sodiation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The ex-situ XRD studies of the alloy anode after first and second sodiation process have been conducted. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows the changes in XRD pattern during sodiation/desodiaiton processes. The formation of poorly crystallized Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase was observed during sodiation, corresponding to the diffraction peaks at 19.1°, 21.3°, and 38.7° (JCPDS no. 741,162). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Similarly, the peaks corresponding to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb were absent in the desodiated electrodes. The constituent FeSb and FeSb<sub>2</sub> alloys progressively transform during cycling into Fe rich Fe<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phase with the extrusion of Sb and the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and amorphous Fe matrix which was not detected in XRD. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Further, the electrochemically inactive Fe matrix would serve as a highly conductive pathway and buffer matrix to aid in the cycling stability of the electrochemically active Sb. Thus, the first sodiation cycle of the Fe–Sb alloy can be described as follows: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/am-2017-13096s_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>During desodiation, the electrochemically inactive Fe serves as the conductive matrix and the following reaction takes place: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/am-2017-13096s_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Ex situ XRD of the electrodes at the cutoff potentials for the first and second cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodiation/desodiation kinetics of the MS_Fe–Sb was investigated by gauging the electrochemical response at increasing scan rates (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). As the scan rates were increased from 0.1 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, significant peak shifts were observed and the corresponding peak currents were plotted based on the power law<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/am-2017-13096s_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span>where <i>i</i><sub><i>p</i></sub> is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Cyclic voltammogram of MS_FeSb anode at different scan rates. (b) log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>) relationship of peaks 1–3. (c) EIS spectra of MS_FeSb fresh electrode and after 80 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows the linear relationship between log(<i>i</i>) and log(<i>v</i>) for the peak currents. The “<i>b</i>” values deduced from the slope of the line are 0.72, 0.73, and 0.56 for peaks 1, 2, and 3, respectively. While “<i>b</i>” value close to 1 reveals pseudocapacitive behavior, b values in the range ∼0.5–0.7 indicate a combination of solid-state diffusion and pseudocapacitive contributions and the excellent rate capability of the MS_Fe–Sb anode possibly stems from this synergistic combination. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Furthermore, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) confirms the formation of a stable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) without significant increase in the charge transfer resistance during cycling as observed from the diameter of the semicircle in the high-frequency region of the Nyquist plot (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c inset). The sloping tail in the low-frequency region can be ascribed to the Warburg impedance and is characteristic for Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion into the electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The excellent cycling performance and rate capability of the melt-spun Fe–Sb alloy motivated us to further investigate its full-cell performance and examine the feasibility of the anode for real-world applications. In this regard, we coupled the MS_FeSb with a sodium manganese oxide (NMO) cathode. The NMO cathode in half-cell assembly delivered a stable capacity of ∼160 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 50 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) with high Coulombic efficiency. The details of the full-cell assembly and the procedure for preparation is given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information Section 1.4</a>. The preliminary investigation of the NMO/Fe–Sb full cell in coin-cell assembly delivers promising performance, as the cell delivered an initial capacity of 350 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on anode mass) and over 99% Coulombic efficiency and retained ∼79% of the initial capacity. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a shows the potential profile of electrodes employed in the full-cell assembly. Full-cell exhibits stable cycling stability (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b) and we demonstrated the potential of full cell to power a white LED lamp (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c). Further tests are in progress to improve the cycling stability of the full cell as well as the performance evaluation in pouch-cell configuration to investigate the prospective scalability of MS_Fe–Sb alloy anode.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13096s_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Full cell performance of MS_FeSb alloy ribbons coupled with NMO cathode: (a) Potential profile and (b) cycling stability at specific current of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Photograph showing LED powered by NMO/MS_FeSb couple.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/asset/images/large/am-2017-13096s_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13096&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, Fe–Sb alloy anodes were fabricated by a scalable and industrially viable melt-spinning process. The alloy anode exhibited exceptional cycling capability and rate performances in sodium-ion half-cell assembly. Nearly 466 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> sodiation capacity was attained at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the second sodiation, with ∼95% capacity retention after 80 cycles. Moreover, the alloy anode displayed promising electrochemical performance in full-cell sodium-ion battery configuration as well as delivered a sodiation capacity of ∼300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the anode) at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, with more than 99% Coulombic efficiency. The results attest the suitability of the MS_Fe–Sb alloy anode for future sodium-ion batteries for practical applications as well as emphasize the need for further extended studies on alloy anodes.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b13096" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b13096</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Details of electrode preparation, electrochemical characterization, and morphological analysis of active materials are incorporated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13096/suppl_file/am7b13096_si_001.pdf">am7b13096_si_001.pdf (1.64 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b13096" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.2085"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Larcher, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.resourpol.2009.05.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yaksic, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tilton, J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">947-958</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research development on sodium-ion batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A New High-Energy Cathode for a Na-Ion Battery with Ultrahigh Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Young-Uk; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Kwon, Hyung-Soon; Kim, Byoungkook; Kim, Jongsoon; Kim, Haegyeom; Kim, Inkyung; Yoo, Han-Ill; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-scale elec. energy storage is a key enabler for the use of renewable energy.  Recently, the room-temp. Na-ion battery has been re-highlighted as an alternative low-cost technol. for this application.  However, significant challenges such as energy d. and long-term stability must be addressed.  Herein, we introduce a novel cathode material, Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7, for Na-ion batteries.  This new material provides an energy d. of ∼600 Wh kg-1, the highest value among cathodes, originating from both the multielectron redox reaction (1.2 e- per formula unit) and the high potential (∼3.8 V vs Na+/Na) of the tailored vanadium redox couple (V3.8+/V5+).  Furthermore, an outstanding cycle life (∼95% capacity retention for 100 cycles and ∼84% for extended 500 cycles) could be achieved, which we attribute to the small vol. change (2.9%) upon cycling, the smallest vol. change among known Na intercalation cathodes.  The open crystal framework with two-dimensional Na diffusional pathways leads to low activation barriers for Na diffusion, enabling excellent rate capability.  We believe that this new material can bring the low-cost room-temp. Na-ion battery a step closer to a sustainable large-scale energy storage system.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1149/1.2221153"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Doeff, M.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. insertion of sodium ions into carbon using solid polymer electrolytes or org. liq. electrolytes is described.  Cells with the configuration Na/P(EO)6NaCF3SO3/CP(EO) (PEO = polyethylene oxide) or Na/liq. electrolyte/C were galvanostatically discharged, charged, and cycled.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Affordable energy storage is crucial for a variety of technologies.  One option is sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) for which, however, suitable anode materials are still a problem.  The authors report on the application of a promising new class of materials, polyoxometalates (POMs), as an anode in NIBs.  Specifically, Na6[V10O28]·16H2O is being synthesized and characterized.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Abstr.: Elemental antimony has recently become an attractive anode material for potential application in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.  I present a first-principles study of the structure-compn. dependence of the Na-Sb system for both sodiation and desodiation processes.  The enthalpy of reaction of x moles of sodium with the hexagonal structure of antimony reveals several stable crystal structures for 0 &lt; x ≤ 3, with variable compn. states for 1.25 &lt; x &lt; 2.75.  The direct and reverse reactions pass through similar states in terms of enthalpy of formation and symmetry representation of the corresponding optimized structures, in particular for x = 1 and x = 3, confirming the two known phases, namely NaSb and Na3Sb.  The calcns. suggest that the optimal compn. range for reversible sodiation of antimony is 1 &lt; x ≤ 3, thus avoiding the global min. at x = 1.  This can help to rationalize the structure-compn. dependence of the electrochem. performance of antimony in Na-ion batteries.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Allan, Phoebe K.; Griffin, John M.; Darwiche, Ali; Borkiewicz, Olaf J.; Wiaderek, Kamila M.; Chapman, Karena W.; Morris, Andrew J.; Chupas, Peter J.; Monconduit, Laure; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">138</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2352-2365</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Operando pair distribution function (PDF) anal. and ex situ 23Na magic-angle spinning solid-state NMR (MAS ssNMR) spectroscopy are used to gain insight into the alloying mechanism of high-capacity antimony anodes for sodium-ion batteries.  Subtraction of the PDF of cryst. NaxSb phases from the total PDF, an approach constrained by chem. phase information gained from 23Na ssNMR in ref. to relevant model compds., identifies two previously uncharacterized intermediate species formed electrochem., a-Na3-xSb (x ≈ 0.4-0.5), a structure locally similar to cryst. Na3Sb (c-Na3Sb) but with significant nos. of sodium vacancies and a limited correlation length, and a-Na1.7Sb, a highly amorphous structure featuring some Sb-Sb bonding.  The first sodiation breaks down the cryst. antimony to form first a-Na3-xSb and, finally, cryst. Na3Sb.  Desodiation results in the formation of an electrode formed of a composite of cryst. and amorphous antimony networks.  We link the different reactivity of these networks to a series of sequential sodiation reactions manifesting as a cascade of processes obsd. in the electrochem. profile of subsequent cycles.  The amorphous network reacts at higher voltages reforming a-Na1.7Sb, then a-Na3-xSb, whereas lower potentials are required for the sodiation of cryst. antimony, which reacts to form a-Na3-xSb without the formation of a-Na1.7Sb. a-Na3-xSb is converted to cryst. Na3Sb at the end of the second discharge.  We find no evidence of formation of NaSb.  Variable temp. 23Na NMR expts. reveal significant sodium mobility within c-Na3Sb, this is a possible contributing factor to the excellent rate performance of Sb anodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA10345F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, L.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Facile synthesis of hierarchical fern leaf-like Sb and its application as an additive-free anode for fast reversible Na-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Liying; Xu, Yang; Li, Yueliang; Dong, Huishuang; Zhou, Min; Zhao, Huaping; Kaiser, Ute; Lei, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchical Sb was successfully fabricated via a very simple and cost-effective electrochem. deposition method. The formation mechanism of the fern leaf-like Sb was also investigated.  The hierarchical Sb exhibits desirable properties for sodium storage, such as high elec. cond. and large surface area.  When used as an additive-free anode for Na-ion batteries, the as-obtained fern leaf-like Sb reveals excellent cycling stability and rate capability.  It can afford a high reversible capacity of 589 mA h g-1 over 150 cycles at 0.5 A g-1 and retain a capacity of 498 mA h g-1 at a high rate of 10 A g-1.  Furthermore, a full cell constructed using P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2//fern leaf-like Sb also displays remarkably stable and robust Na-storage performance, which includes a high capacity retention of 70% after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g-1 and a large capacity of 370 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance of fern leaf-like Sb can be attributed to its morphol. and structural features that ensure fast ion and electron transport and a stable electrode structure.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b01761"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Vail, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the room-temp. rechargeable sodium-ion battery has emerged as an attractive alternative energy storage soln. for large-scale deployment, major challenges toward practical sodium-ion battery technol. remain including identification and engineering of anode materials that are both technol. feasible and economical.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb@C coaxial nanotubes as a superior long-life and high-rate anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhiming; Yu, Xin-Yao; Lou, Xiong Wen; Paik, Ungyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony (Sb) is an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a high theor. capacity of 660 mAh g-1.  However, its practical application is greatly hindered by the rapid capacity fading which is largely due to the large vol. expansion during sodiation.  Tuning the morphol. and structure at the nanoscale or using carbonaceous materials as the buffer layer is essential to address this issue.  Here, a facile carbon-coating coupled with a thermal-redn. strategy has been developed to synthesize unique Sb@C coaxial nanotubes.  With different annealing time, the hollow space and the amt. of Sb inside the tube can be easily tuned by the partial evapn. of Sb.  The as-obtained Sb@C nanotubes exhibit excellent sodium storage properties.  The remarkable electrochem. performance results from the unique coaxial nanoarchitecture.  Specifically, it delivers a high specific capacity of 407 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 240 cycles.  Furthermore, a stable capacity of 240 mAh g-1 can be retained at 1.0 A g-1 even after 2000 cycles.  Most importantly, high capacities of 350 mAh g-1 and 310 mAh g-1 can be achieved at large current densities of 10 and 20 A g-1, resp., which represents the best rate performance among the reported Sb-based anode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/am507436u"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jackson, E. The influence of the carbonaceous matrix on the electrochem. performance of the alloy anode is investigated in half-cell assembly.  Amongst, FeSn2-Graphite composite exhibits excellent cycling stability with a reversible capacity of 333 mAh g-1 obtained after 100 cycles at a specific current of 100 mA g-1.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High Anode Performance of in Situ Formed Cu2Sb Nanoparticles Integrated on Cu Foil via Replacement Reaction for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Liubin; Wang, Chenchen; Zhang, Ning; Li, Fujun; Cheng, Fangyi; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A scalable and binder-free Cu2Sb/Cu electrode has been synthesized via replacement reaction as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The thickness of Cu2Sb formed on Cu foil can be facilely tuned by adjusting the concn. of Sb3+ and reaction time.  A high capacity of 318.4 mAh g-1 is obtained at 0.08 A g-1, and a capacity retention of 98.5% is also maintained after 200 cycles at 0.8 A g-1.  The reaction mechanism of the as-synthesized Cu2Sb/Cu is investigated by using ex situ X-ray diffraction and transmission electron microscopy.  The porous Cu2Sb nanoparticle film and its intrinsic contact with the Cu foil result in the good electrochem. performance.
